Title: Tobacco/Second-Hand Smoke Counseling (Not Started)
Topic: How to Quit {Not Started)
Point: Repetition (Not Started) .
Description: It often takes multiple attempts to successfully quit smoking. C« ity tobacca i increase the likeli of success.
Cognitive behavioral activities can be useful in learning tocope with smoking urges.
Withdrawal symptoms typically peak within 1-2 weeks and may persist for months.
Lifestyle changes, such as avoiding activities associated with smoking, may be helpful.
Summary: Learning progress - nat on file.
Point: Treatment Options (Not Started)
Description: Quitting smoking may be easier with help.
Options available to help quit include:
counseling
antidepressant medication
nicotine replacement therapy
caping and encouragement strategies
cammunity support groups
Encourage discussion of options to determine what might work best.
Summary: Learning progress - not on file.
Paint: Managing Roadblocks (Not Started)
Description: Both extrinsic and intrinsic factors can trigger the desire to smoke.
Extrinsic factors may include:
being around other smokers.
drinking alcohol
stress
cost of medications to quit
lifestyle changes required
Intrinsic factors may include:
withdrawal symptoms, such as feeling ill, when trying to quit
addictive nature ofsmoking
fear offailure
weight gain
lack of support or caping skills
depression
Summary: Learning progress - not on file.
Topic: Relevance of Quitting (Not Started)
Point: Tobacca/Smoking Risks {Not Started)
Description: Continuing to smoke exposes the smoker to bath acute and long-term risks.
Acute risks include:
delayed wound healing
immune system impairment
increased risk of respiratory infection
shortness ofbreath
asthma orCOPD exacerbation
infertility
sexual performance issues
Long-terms risks include:
heart disease
stroke
cancer
breathing problems
ulcers
gum disease
fingernail or teeth stains

lower bloed pressure

increased risk of low birth weight in newborns
increased risk of Sudden Infant Death Syndrome (SIDS)
asthma in babies and children
harm to pregnancy
increased susceptibility to respiratory infections
elevated rates of cardiovascular disease and cancer

Education
Tittle: Tohacco/Second-Hand Smoke Counseling (Not Started)
Topic: How to Quit (Not Started)
Point: Repetition {Not Started) | . -
Description; It often takes multiple attempts to successfully quit smoking, Community tobacco cessation programs increase thelikelihood ofsuccess.
Cognitive behavioral activities can be useful in leaming to cope with smoking urges.
Withdrawal symptoms typically peak within 1-2 weeks and may persist for months.
Lifestyle changes, such as avoiding activities associated with smoking, may be helpful.
Summary: Learning progress - not on file.
Point: Treatment Options (Nat Started) _
Description: Quitting smoking may be easier with help.
Options available to help quit include:
counseling
antidepressant medication
nicotine replacement therapy
coping and encouragement strategies
community support groups
Encourage discussion of aptions to determine what might work best.
Summary: Learning progress ~ not on file.
Point: Managing Roadblocks (Not Started)
Description: Both extrinsic and intrinsic factors can trigger the desire to smoke.
Extrinsic factors may include:
being around other smokers
diinking alcohol
stress
cost of medications to quit
lifestyle changes required
Intrinsic factors may include:
withdrawal symptoms, such as feeling ill, when trying to quit
addictive nature of smoking
fear offailure
weight gain
lack of support or coping skills
depression
Summary: Learning progress - not on file.
Topic: Relevance of Quitting (Not Started)
Point: Tobacco/Smoking Risks (Not Started}
Description: Continuing to smoke exposes the smoker to both acute and long-term risks.
Acute risks include:
delayed wound healing
immune system impairment
increased risk of respiratory infection
shortness ofbreath
asthma or COPD exacerbation
infertility
sexual performance issues
Long-terms risks include:
heart disease
stroke
cancer
breathing problems
ulcers
gum disease
fingernail or teeth stains

Description: Secondhand smoke affects those exposed as well.
It has been associated with:
increased risk of low birth weight in newborns
increased risk of Sudden Infant Death Syndrome (SIDS)
asthma in babies and children
harm to pregnancy
increased susceptibility to respiratory infections
elevated rates of cardiovascular disease and cancer

Point: Repetition (Not Started) _ . . . . . ae .
Description: It often takes multiple attempts to successfully quit smoking. C ity tobacco ion p increase the likeli of success.
Cognitive behavioral activities can be useful in learning to cope with smoking urges.
Withdrawal symptoms typically peak within 1-2weeks and may persist for months,
Lifestyle changes, such as avoiding activities associated with smoking, may be helpful.
Summary: Learning progress - not on file.
Point: Treatment Options (Nat Started)
Description: Quitting smoking may beeasier with help.
Options available to help quit include:
counseling
antidepressant medication
nicotine replacement therapy
coping and encouragement strategies
community support groups :
Encourage discussion of options to determine what might work best.
Summary: Learning progress - noton file.
Point: Managing Roadblocks (Not Started)
Description: Both extrinsic and intrinsic factors can trigger the desire tosmoke.
Extrinsic factors may include:
being around other smokers
drinking aleoho!
stress
cost cf medications to quit
lifestyle changes required
Intrinsic factors may include:
withdrawal symptoms, such as feeling ill, when trying to quit
addictive nature of smoking
fear offailure
weight gain
lack ofsupport or coping skills
depression
Summary: Learning progress - not onfile.
Topic: Relevance of Quitting (Not Started)
Point: Tobacco/Smoking Risks {Not Started)
Description: Continuing to smoke exposes thesmoker to both acute and long-term risks.
Acute risks include:
delayed wound healing
immune system impairment
increased risk of respiratory infection
shortness of breath
asthma or COPD exacerbation
infertility
sexual performance issues
Lang-terms risks include:
heart disease
stroke
breathing problems.
ulcers
gum disease
fingernail or teeth stains

Point: Tobacco/Smoking Risks (Not Started)
Description: Continuing tosmoke exposes thesmoker to both acute and long-term risks.
Acute risks include:
delayed wound healing
immune system impairment
increased risk of respiratory infection
shortness of breath
asthma or COPD exacerbation
infertility
sexual performance issues
Long-terms risks include:
heart disease
stroke
breathing problems
ulcers
gum disease
fingernail or teeth stains
inability to work or long-term disability

Point: Rewards of Quitting {Not Started)
Description: Even with long-term tobacco use, there are benefits to quitting today or as soon as possible.
These benefits include:
lower blood pressure
improved oxygenation
enhanced sense oftaste and smell
improved performance of physical activity
feeling better physically
improved physical appearance and health
improved sense of wellbeing
satting a good example for children
financial savings
healthier babies and children

Point: Risks toOthers {Not Started}
Description: Secondhand smoke affects those exposed as well.
Ithas been associated with:
increased risk of low birth weight in newborns
increased risk of Sudden Infant Death Syndrome (SIDS)
asthma in babies and children
harm to pregnancy
increased susceptibility to respiratory infections
elevated rates ofcardiovascular disease and cancer

ED Provider Notes - ED Notes
Service: Emergency Medicine
Creation Time: 3/30/2019 5:43 PM
Author Type: Physician
Status: Addendum
Patient is a 35-year-old female who presents the emergency department complaining of a severe headache
located in the back of her head for the past 2-3 days. The patient states that she went to urgent care earlier
today and was told that she needed to come to the emergency department for a CT scan due to her symptoms.
She states that the pain is located on the crown of her head, and is making her scalp very tender to touch. She
states that she is unable to even brush her hair due to this pain. She has never had these symptoms in the
past and has nc history of migraine headaches. She denies any changes in vision, slurred speech, fever, chills

More Information
Occipital Neuralgia
Occipital neuralgia is a type of headache that causes episodes of very bad pain inthe back of your head. Pain
from occipital neuralgia may spread (radiate) to other parts of your head. The pain is usually brief and often
goes away after you rest and relax.
These headaches may be caused by irritation ofthe nerves that leave your spinal cord high up in your neck, just
below the base of your skull (eccipital nerves). Your occipital nerves transmit sensations from the back ofyour
head, the top ofyour head, and the areas behind your ears.
What are the causes?
Occipital neuralgia can occur without any known cause (primary headache syndrome). In other cases, occipital
neuralgia is caused by pressure on or irritation of one ofthe two occipital nerves. Causes of occipital nerve
compression or irritation include:
Wear and tear of the vertebrae in the neck (osteoarthritis).
Neck injury.
Disease of the disks that separate the vertebrae.
Tumors.
Gout.
Infections.
Diabetes,
Swollen blood vessels that put pressure on the occipital nerves.
Muscle spasm in the neck.
What are the signs or symptoms?
Pain is the main symptom of occipital neuralgia. It usually starts in the back ofthe head but may also be felt in
other areas supplied by the occipital nerves. Pain is usually on one side but may be on both sides. You may
have:
e Brief episodes ofvery bad pain that is burning, stabbing, shocking, or shooting.
e Pain behind the eye.
e Pain triggered by neck movement or hair brushing.
e Scalp tenderness.
e Aching in the back of the head between episodes ofvery bad pain.
How is this diagnosed?
Your health care provider may diagnose occipital neuralgia based on your symptoms and a physical exam.
During the exam, the health care provider may push on areas supplied bythe occipital nerves to see ifthey are
painful. Some tests may also be done to help in making the diagnosis. These may include:
¢ Imaging studies of the upper spinal cord, such as an MRI or CT scan. These may show compression or
spinal cord abnormalities.
Generated on 6/11/19 9:59 AM Page 190

SF aris HEALTH Baptist Health Corbin Mills, Amy
oe 4Trillium Way MRN: [PHONE-417], DOB: [DOB-1], Sex: F
Corbin KY 40701-8727 Adm: 3/30/2019, D/C: 3/30/2019
More Information (continued)
e Nerve block. You will get an injection of numbing medicine (local anesthetic) near the occipital nerve to
see ifthis relieves pain.
How is this treated?
Treatment may begin with simple measures, such as:
Rest.
Massage.
Heat.
Over-the-counter pain relievers.
If these measures do not work, you may need other treatments, including:
¢ Medicines such as:
0 Prescription-strength anti-inflammatory medicines.
oO Muscle relaxants.
o Antiseizure medicines.
o Antidepressants.
e Steroid injection. This involves injections oflocal anesthetic and strong anti-inflammatory drugs
(steroids).
e Pulsed radiofrequency. Wires are implanted to deliver electrical impulses that block pain signals from
the occipital nerve.
e Physical therapy.
¢ Surgery to relieve nerve pressure.
Follow these instructions at home:
Take all medicines as directed by your health care provider.
Avoid activities that cause pain.
Rest when you have an attack of pain.
Try gentle massage or a heating pad to relieve pain.
Work with a physical therapist to learn stretching exercises you can do at home.
Try a different pillow or sleeping position.
Practice good posture.
Try to stay active. Get regular exercise that does not cause pain. Ask your health care provider to suggest
safe exercises for you.
¢ Keep all follow-up visits as directed by your health care provider. This is important.
Contact a health care provider if:
* Your medicine is not working.
¢ You have new or worsening symptoms.

More Information (continued)
General Headache Without Cause
A headache is pain or discomfort felt around the head or neck
area. There are many causes and types of headaches. In some
cases, the cause may not-be found.
Follow these instructions at home:
Managing pain
Take over-the-counter and prescription medicines only as told by
your doctor.
Lie down ina dark, quiet room when you have a headache.
Ifdirected, apply ice tothe head and neck area:
Put ice in a plastic bag.
Place a towel between your skin and the bag.
Leave the ice on for 20 minutes, 2—3 times per day.
Use a heating pad or hot shower to apply heat to the head and
neck area as told by your doctor.
° Keep lights dim ifbright lights bother you or make your
headaches worse.
Eating and drinking
« Eat meals on a regular schedule.
« lLessen how much alcohol you drink.
« Lessen how much caffeine you drink, or stop drinking caffeine.
General instructions
e Keep all follow-up visits as told by your doctor. This is important.
e Keep ajournal to find out ifcertain things bring on headaches. For example, write down:
o What you eat and drink.
o How much sleep you get.
o Any change to your diet or medicines.
Relax by getting a massage or doing other relaxing activities.
Lessen stress.
Sit up straight. Do not tighten (tense) your muscles.
Do not use tobacco products. This includes cigarettes, chewing tobacco, or e-cigarettes. Ifyou need help
quitting, ask your doctor.
Exercise regularly as told by your doctor.
« Get enough sleep. This often means 7-9 hours of sleep.
Contact a doctor if:
¢ Your symptoms are not helped by medicine.
* You have a headache that feels different than the other headaches.
e You feel sick to your stomach (nauseous) or you throw up (vomit).

Title: Tobacco/Second-Hand Smoke Counseling (Not Started)
Topic: How te Quit (Not Started)
Point: Repetition (Not Started)
Description: Itoften takes multiple attempts to successfully quit smoking. C tobacco pi Increase the Ilkelihood of success,
Cognitive behavioral actlvitles can be useful in learning to cope with smoking urges,
Withdrawal symptoms typically peak within 1-2 weeks and may persist for months.
Lifestyle changes, such as avoiding activities assoctated with smoking, may behelpful.
Summary: Learning progress - noton file.
Polnt; Treatment Options (Not Started)
Description: Quitting smoking may be easier with help.
Options available to help quit include:
counseling
antidepressant medication
hicotine replacement therapy
coping and encouragement strategles
community support groups
Encourage discussion ofoptions to determine what might work best.
Summary: Learning progress - not on file.
Point: Managing Roadblocks (Not Started)
Description: Both extrinsic and intrinsic factors can trigger the desire to smoke.
Extrinsic factors may include:
being around other smokers
drinking alcohol
stress
cost of medications toquit
lifestyle changes required
Intrinsic factors may include:
withdrawal symptoms, such as feeling ill, when trying to quit
addictive nature of smoking
fear of failure
weight gain
lack of support or coping skills
depression
Summary: Learning progress - noton file.
Topic: Relevance of Quitting (Not Started)
Point: Tobacco/Smoking Risks (Not Started)
Description: Continuing to smoke exposes the smoker to both acute and long-term risks.
Acute risks include:
delayed wound healing
immune system impairment
increased risk of respiratory infection
shortness of breath
asthma or COPD exacerbation
infertility
sexual performance issues
Long-terms risks include:
heart disease
stroke
cancer
breathing problems
ulcers
gum disease
fingernail or teeth stains
inability to work or long-term disability
Summary: Learning progress - not onfile.

More Information
Muscle Strain
A muscle strain is an injury that happens when a muscle is stretched longer than normal. This can happen
during a fail, sports, or lifting. This can tear some muscle fibers. Usually, recovery from muscle strain takes 1-2
weeks. Complete healing normally takes 5-6 weeks.
This condition is first treated with PRICE therapy. This involves:
Protecting your muscle from being injured again.
Resting your injured muscle.
Icing your injured muscle.
Applying pressure (compression) to your injured muscle. This may be done witha splint or elastic
bandage.
¢ Raising (elevating) your injured muscle.
Your doctor may also recommend medicine forpain.
Follow these instructions at home:
Ifyou have a splint:
Wear the splint as told by your doctor. Take it off only as told by your doctor,
Loosen the splint ifyour fingers or toes tingle, get numb, or turn cold and blue.
Keep the splint clean.
Ifthe splint is not waterproof:
© Do not let it get wet.
°o Cover it with a watertight covering when you take a bath or a shower.
Managing pain, stiffness, and swelling
¢ I£directed, put ice on your injured area.
o Ifyou have a removable splint, take it off as told by your doctor.
o Put ice in a plastic bag.
© Place a towel between your skin and the bag.
o Leave the ice on for 20 minutes, 2-3 times a day.
* Move your fingers ortoes often. This helps to avoid stiffness and lessen swelling.
* Raise your injured area above the level of your heart while you are sitting or lying down.

¢ You have more pain or swelling in your injured area,
Get help right away if:
¢ You have any ofthese problems in your injured area:
°o You have numbness.
o You have tingling.
© You losea lot of strength.
Summary
¢ A muscle strain is an injury that happens when a muscle is stretched longer than normal.
¢ This condition is first treated with PRICE therapy. This includes protecting, resting, icing, adding
pressure, and raising your injury.
* Limit your activity. Rest your injured muscle as told by your doctor. Your doctor may say that gentle
movements are okay.
¢ Warm up before you exercise. This helps to prevent more muscle strains.
This information is not intended to replace advice given to you by your health care provider. Make sure you
discuss any questions you have with your health care provider.
